this is immensely resented by the true of the


tribe: we have earned our place here, feel worthy and
honored in our station, would prefer not to be
represented by worthless fakers: one can’t give up
what one
never had.




























































118


ACTION




Henry Baroyan cut between the Caddy and the Porsche, opened it up to a gentle 80, sucked on his cigar, inhaling, thinking, maybe I’ll have some luck today, I sure as shit need it. The BMW was 5 years old but it still tooled well. He’d paid 88 bucks for his new license plate tag but then he’d lost it. He always lost his license tags. He always lost everything. Even the Pulitzer Prize. Ten years ago when he’d been riding high he had turned it down, stating that the only prize a writer needed was already his.


He’d married the same woman twice. Lost $350,000 at the track. Couldn’t pay his taxes. They took the house, they took everything. They just left him the car and the typer and the wife. Taxes owed: $440,000. How did things turn out that way? Henry used to pay 6 percent interest on the back taxes, now he was paying 16. And he was the guy who used to write stories about starving and writing in a small room. How well off he’d been then! Now he owed more money than he’d ever earn. He was broke, the government was broke, the world was broke. Who had the god damned money?


“You going to the fucking track again?” Traci had asked. Traci was his wife.


“I got to, babe. It makes the typer run. I’ve got to have the action.” “You can write without gambling. Don’t be a fool. Don’t get in any
deeper.”
“What difference does it make if I owe $440,000 or $940,000?”
“The difference is $500,000.”
“Smart girl. I’m going.”
“You’re a total asshole!”


And the worst was, he couldn’t write anymore. Larry Simpson now wrote his stuff. Nothing near the way Henry used to write. Larry was a hack. But the Baroyan name was still saleable. Larry was his ghost. And Larry got 40%.


Maybe some day it will come back to me, thought Henry. Maybe a real good day at the track will bring it back.


Speaking of the track, he was there. He was at parking. The attendants all knew him. He pulled up to the fat one with the transistor radio.
















119
The attendant was listening to Madonna. Maybe some day Madonna would have her ass in the wringer. The guy pulled out a Valet ticket and Henry gave him 6 bucks.
“You killin’ ’em, Champ?” the fat guy asked.


“Fair meet,” Henry answered, then pulled out and followed the blue line to Valet.


When he got there he left his car without picking up a claim stub. They knew that car. Only one guy drove a black 79 BMW with the front fog lights ripped off.


The attendants always parked his car nearby and when they saw him coming out just before the last race (he liked to get out before the crowd) one of the fellows would have it running and waiting for him. They didn’t know he was a fucking bum. On his losing days he gave them a fiver for the service. When he won, it was more. Most days they got a fiver.


One of the parking attendants saw him. It was Bob. Bob had a little more going than the others.


“Hey, Hank,” he said, “if this isn’t my day, I hope it’s yours.” “Thanks, Bob, maybe we’ll both tally.”


“Sure,” he answered, “and maybe jackrabbits will go scuba-diving.” Henry showed his pass at the clubhouse window and walked on through.


He picked up his program and his Racing Form. He walked straight through the clubhouse and into the grandstand area. He belonged with the poor, he was poorer than any of them.


He found his favorite bar. Rusty was behind the wood. Rusty was a player too.
“Vodka-7, Rusty.”
“O.K…. Looks like a great card today, Hank.”
“I haven’t split the Form yet.” “Listen, I’ve got a good one for you.” “Don’t want to hear it.”


“It’s in the 3rd, a maiden race. You know how they fix those. They win by ten.”
“Don’t give me any black-dot workout horse. Those suck.”


“Hey, they been working this one in the dark, even before the clockers get in. It’ll pay at least 15-to-one. Nobody knows.”
Henry drained his vodka.
“How come you know?”
“Somebody owes me a favor.”












120


“Jesus, you too?”


Rusty leaned forward and whispered, “Red Window in the 3rd.” Henry laid down the money for the drink, plus tip. “Bullshit.”


“No,” said Rusty, “not this time. And here’s one on me.”
He poured Henry a refill.
“Thanks, Rusty.”
“How’s the writing coming?”
“It could be better,” Henry answered, draining his drink.
He moved off then, walking toward the escalator which would take him to the upper deck. At the bottom of the escalator, standing in a patch of sun, was an old black fellow with large kindly eyes.


“Hey, man,” he said, “don’t I know you?” “Not unless you’re the IRS.”


“No, I’ve seen you on the tube, or maybe in the movies.” “I was in a porno flick once, long ago.” “No, man, I know your face.”
“You’re thinking of somebody else.”
Henry walked past and got on the escalator.


“No, man,” the old guy called after him, “you can’t fool me, I think I seen you in some tv series!”
Henry looked back. “You’re thinking of somebody else, bro…”


On the second deck Henry went to the service area and got a large beer. He took the beer down to a seat and took a hit. He gagged. The shit was green. It needed a while in the air. He split the Form, checked the first race. He wouldn’t suck on the closers and class drops today. He needed some early foot. Something with lick. If you’ve got to lose, lose in front.


He noticed the young man moving toward him. Then he noticed the legs. The legs just stood there. Henry didn’t look up. Then there was a voice. A young voice.


“Pardon me, I don’t like to disturb you, but aren’t you Henry Baroyan, the writer?”
“No, I’m his cousin, Andre.”


“Ah, come on! I know you! We’re studying you in Modern Lit at Long Beach State.”
“I’m just a horseplayer.”


“You’re one of my favorite writers, man.” “Yeah? Who are some of the others?”












121


“Burroughs, Ginsberg, Genet, Bowles…” “Get lost.”


“Listen, Mr. Baroyan, do me a favor. Just autograph a program for me?” “All right. Where?”


“Here…on the front…and make one of your little drawings. You know, those drawings you do.”


The kid handed Henry his program. The boy was white and soft, lost in the dream before the reality. Nice boy, nice boy…


“Don’t bet on a horse that shits as it nears the starting gate,” he wrote. Then he found a space in the margin, signed his name, drew a picture of a nag disposing of some waste. Handed the program back.
“Really, thanks…” said the kid, and then he was gone.
Maybe, thought Henry, I can write a story about the racetrack. That fucking Larry Simpson, all he ever wrote about was Mardi Gras, or rattlesnake races, or drownings off the coast of Argentina, or politicians in love with Irish terriers. Also, ice cream dreams of a free and wonderful world.


Then—just like that—it was time for the first race. Henry moved toward the lines where they stood: the lonely and the demented, the helplessly ugly with their run-down heels. And those faces so long ago robbed of everything but a determination to go on without hope, melody, or even the slightest expectation of victory.


When death comes for us, thought Henry, it will spit us out like a bone long cleaned and finished, dry and hard and…what? And, nothing.


Then, as Henry was standing in line, up rushed Marsden. Marsden used to be a parimutuel clerk but he had somehow fornicated with his machine in an illegal way and had lost his position. Still, he wasn’t barred from the track. And Marsden had done Henry many good turns, especially at the harness meets where the hot good money often dropped in so late and so fast that it was almost impossible to get in on the action. Marsden would punch out a fifty win ticket for him, looking down to the end of the long line where Henry stood and signaled the horse’s number. Marsden and the harness races had been good to Henry.


Marsden wore tailor-made clothes, appeared to be always happy, often laughing…a charming fucker who had much trouble with women. More so even than Henry.
Marsden stood there in his fine threads.














122


“Hey, baby!”


He had somebody with him. Another body in great threads. “This is my attorney.”


The world was good. They shook hands. The world was good, and a joke. The attorney moved away toward the bar.


“Listen, baby,” said Marsden, “I know you’re a player. I know you know who’s going to come in. Put me on to it.”


“This isn’t the harness meet, Mars…” “Fuck that. Put me on to it.” “I prefer the 9.”


“That’s it! That’s it! I HEARD! They told me it can’t miss! Listen, let me have fifty, I came out here short and then I heard about the 9. Let me have fifty, please!”


“I’m up against the wall, Mars. All I can lift is a 20.” “O.K….”
Henry gave Marsden the 20 and Marsden was gone.


Henry was about 3 bettors away from the teller when the announcement came over the loudspeaker, “By order of the stewards, Happy Hour has been scratched.”
Happy Hour was the 9 horse.
Fuck, thought Henry. Then he was facing the parimutuel clerk.
“Twenty win, 5,” he said.


He went out to watch the race. He saw it, and then he heard it: “Hot Watch was left at the gate!”
Hot Watch was the 5.


Henry walked around as the race went on. He thought maybe he could find Marsden. Maybe Marsden would give the 20 back. It was a matter of principle. And a 20 might save his ass. Well, maybe not save it but help a bit in the impossible fight.


But he couldn’t find Marsden. He couldn’t find anybody who looked even a little bit like Marsden. They didn’t even look like themselves. Everybody looked like a flattened animal that had been rolled over by a street paver. Even the women. Especially the women.


He moved deeper into the crowd as they stood intent with the running of the race.


The women of the poor. God, how unkind of him to think about them that way. Many probably had little geranium plants they loved. And doper offspring, caught pushing, now rotting in remote cells all over the world, where the rats never smiled.








123


Well, shit, the day wasn’t over. No day was over until it was over. Hope sprang eternal like the poison mushroom.


Then the race was over. They had run in a 26-to-one shot, a speed burner up from Caliente. Most of the people didn’t look too happy. There were many Mexicans and blacks in the crowd, hoping to score, hoping to break their chains. They never would. They were only adding more links to their chains. The whites seemed most pitiful, flabby, with their deadly angry eyes. Most were male. One thing about the white male, though, he was wonderful material for the writer. You could write anything you wanted to about the white American male and nobody ever protested. Not even the white American male. But if you wrote anything disagreeable about any other race or class or gender the critics and the public became furious and your hate mail stacked up, although book sales didn’t seem to drop off. When they hated you, they had to read you. They were aching to see what you would say next about their world. While the white American male didn’t give a damn what you said about him because he ruled the world—at the moment, anyhow.


A big white guy was moving toward Henry, smiling just a bit. Henry started to move off.
“Hey!” the guy said.
Henry turned and waited.
The big guy moved up and stopped, standing a little too close. Henry backed up.
“I’m Monty Edwards,” he said, “I’m a jock’s agent.”


“Well, that’s fine,” said Henry, “but I can’t ride for you, I scale 225.” “Aren’t you Henry Baroyan, the writer?” “Well, yes.”


“Bernard Loft is a trainer. He reads your stuff and likes it. He’d like to meet you.”
“Tell him I said ‘hello.’”
“Aw come on, don’t be a turd, he wants to meet you.”
“All right, where is he?”
“Follow me.”


Henry followed Edwards to the east end of the grandstand, past the at-tendant and into the box seats. The box seat section was another matter: filled with owners, trainers, jockey’s agents, x-jocks and the jetsam of the racing world. They looked a little calmer than the grandstand crowd. They were better dressed and didn’t move












124
about as much. They stood in small groups, talking leisurely.


Henry followed Edwards down to a box. A guy stood up. He was dressed in great threads. His shoes sparkled. Silver blue flowing tie.


“Baroyan,” he said, “I’m Bernard Loft. Your work has given me a great deal of pleasure.”
“Thanks.”
They shook hands.
“Listen,” said Loft, “I’d like to repay you a little for the good reading you’ve given me.”
“No hot tips, please.”


“No, no, I’ve got a horse going in this race. I expect to win and I’d like you to come down to the winner’s circle to be photographed with us.”
“Who’s your horse?”
“Highwater.”
“He’s a closer. How ya gonna catch Wormwood?” “We’ll make a run at him. We should get up in time.” “I can’t see it, Loft. But luck to you, of course.”


“Now, if you’ll pardon me,” said Loft, “I have to get down to the walking ring.”
“Sure.”
Henry sat down with Edwards.
Edwards noticed a woman in a large white hat, white designer dress, red scarf, pigeon face with outsized mouse eyes. He smiled at her.


“Hello, Mrs. Carrington. How is everything going?” “Fine, Monty. Only we lost our filly yesterday.” “Oh?”
“Yes, she was claimed in the 5th.”
“Oh, I’m sorry. Maybe you can get her back.”
“Yes, we intend to claim her right back the next time out.”


Fucking rich, thought Henry, they get $40,000 for a horse and it makes them sad.


“All right,” Henry said to Edwards, “I’m going to walk around a bit, then bet.”
“Who do you like?”
“Wormwood.”
“You’re not betting on Bernard’s horse?” “No, he’s got to come from too far back.” “Bernard says Highwater will come in.”












125


“We’ll see.”


“You coming back here to watch the race?” “O.K.”


Henry walked up the aisle and into the betting area. The windows were open and there were no lines at the moment. He placed 40 win on Worm-wood. Wormwood was reading 7-to-2 and figured to go off about there.


I should be home typing, thought Henry. This writer’s block isn’t going to last forever.


He walked over to the bar and got a vodka-7. He glanced about. The women all seemed younger, lithe, full of little jokes in this privileged section of the track. Even the older women looked pretty good. It saddened Henry. Why did the women of the poor have to look so bad? It wasn’t fair. But what was fair? Had there ever been any fair time for the little guy? All that shit they were fed about democracy and opportunity was just to keep them from burning down the palace. Sure, once in a while a guy climbed out of the rubble and made it. But for each one of them there were hundreds of thousands down on skid row or in jail or in the madhouses, or suicided or drugged or drunk. And many more working pitiful low-paying jobs, throwing away their years for the merest subsistence.


Slavery had not been eliminated, it had only been expanded to include nine-tenths of the population. Everywhere. Holy shit.


Henry finished his drink and walked back to the box. Edwards had gone off somewhere and he had the box to himself. That’s the way it should be. Space. Solace. Far from the madding crowd. Yes.


While waiting, he checked the 3rd race, remembering the tip from Rusty on Red Window. Henry had never seen a tip come in. But the problem with a tip was you had to bet it because if it came in and you didn’t bet it you had to kick yourself in the ass. But the really wise thing to do was not to bet Red Window. But was he wise enough?


They were putting them in the gate for the 2nd race. Wormwood had dropped to three-to-one and Highwater was reading 2. Edwards was back. He took the seat next to Henry.
“When’s your next book coming out?” he asked.
“Any time now.”


The bell rang and they were off. It was 6 furlongs. Wormwood went to an easy lead, had a length-and-a-half with a first fraction of 24. Not bad. Down the backstretch he was still running easy and increased his














126
lead to 2. He glided around the curve and opened up on the stretch. He now had three-and-one-half lengths and was galloping, ears pricked, he was silky, not a drop of sweat showing.


Then, Highwater made his move. He was closing, closing. Wormwood still had 2 lengths, then one-and-one-half, then one, then one-half, and the wire was rushing up at them. Pincay was whipping hell out of Highwater. Hell and Highwater. At the wire it looked like one horse with 8 legs. It was a nose either way.
The photo sign went up.
“Looked like a dead heat,” said Edwards.
“I think it was Wormwood,” Henry said. “In these tight ones the inside horse usually gets it.”


“The only thing is,” said Edwards, “Pincay is a living fiend at the wire.” Just give me this one, Henry whispered to the gods, just give me this one and everything else is going to smooth out. I’m not asking much, just give me the nose. A man can get tired, you know. A man can get very very tired.
Just the nose. This one time. Once. Now.
The sign went up.
Highwater.
I need the typewriter, thought Henry. That way I can make things come out the way I want them to. And now, what am I going to do about Red Window?
Then Bernard Loft was there.
“Follow me, Baroyan, please.”


“Nice run. Congratulations. I never thought you’d catch me…” “I really don’t like the horse but I found a nice spot for him.”


Henry followed Loft down a stairway, then they went through the gate and out into the winner’s circle. Highwater was just entering. Pincay dis-mounted.
“Nice ride, Laffit, thank you,” Loft said.
“Thank you,” Pincay responded, “you really had him ready.”


Pincay seemed like a real gentleman. There was no swagger to him. He was a class act.


The owners and the friends of the owners stood in a long line on one side of the horse, there must have been ten or eleven of them. Pincay stood in the center. On the other side of the horse was the groom, Loft and Henry.
“Look at the camera,” Loft said to Henry.














127


There was a flash and everybody began to move off.


“I’ll find you tomorrow and give you a photo of this,” Loft said. “Thanks much, Loft, this has been my biggest thrill and I don’t get thrilled
too often.”
“You’re a fine writer,” said Loft.
Henry found a cigarette, lit it and walked off.


He went through the gate and up the stairway and then he was back in the betting area.
Little did Loft know that he was washed-up. Finis.
Henry walked out of the box section and back to the grandstand, back to the poor. He found a seat in the grandstand.


Some of the suckers were drinking warm cans of beer they had brought in. The heat of the day and the heat of losing could take the chill out of anything. After just two races, their faces were already sweaty and doomed. They were waiting on the impossible and the impossible seldom arrived. Only in the movies, baby, only on the tv tube. That’s where some of them got that tiny boost that kept them going.


They ought to close this fucking place down, thought Henry, but they never will. The State gets rich on the taxes.


Henry checked the board on Red Window. He had a morning line of 15 and on the first flash he opened at 18. Not bad. Maybe the word had been kept dark for a change. The horse was unraced, showed mediocre works in the Form. Also the jock had a low win percentage. None of the sheets tabbed the horse. There didn’t seem to be a rumble.
Well?
Henry sat.
The horse dropped to 15, then 14, then rose back to 17.


Six or seven rows down a fat Mexican woman was trying to keep a screaming baby quiet. The woman’s husband was already drunk on beer. He wasn’t even reading the Form. He just sat there sucking on a beer-can while the baby wailed. The woman seemed embarrassed, almost in agony, trying to keep the baby quiet. She was nicely dressed for the track. She had a blue bow in her hair. The husband had on an L.A. Dodgers baseball cap. He was wearing it backwards. The baby wailed on. The guy finished his beer, tossed it, cracked a new one.


If I ever go to hell, thought Henry, this is what it will be like. Or, maybe I’ve already died and gone to hell.


Up above Henry, in the last row, was a gang of young men. They had their shirts off and were drunker than most of the other patrons.








128
They had a transistor radio on loud, very loud, to a rock station. They ar-gued and cursed over their selections. They apparently liked to use words like “shit” and “fuck” and “cocksucker.” But their voices hadn’t matured as yet—the voices cracked from time to time and there was an almost female tone to the sound. The words “shit” and “fuck” and “cocksucker” echoed with a false bravado.


At the post parade Red Window was reading 12. At 7 minutes to post he was reading 14.
Henry went down to a betting window. The lines were still long.
He got his bet down with 4 minutes to go. 50 to win.
The horse read 11.
Henry climbed back upstairs and sat down again. By the time he got to his seat, Red Window was reading 8.


The odds began to fall with each flick of the board. 8…7…6… The horses were nearing the gate. 5…


People were running out of the grandstand toward the betting windows, hollering, “Red Window!”


I ought to cancel my god damned ticket, thought Henry, I’ve never seen a big flash-down win yet. But maybe this is the day? Maybe it’s a super horse? I better let it ride.
9-to-2…
4…
7-to-2…
They were in the gate. Thank Christ.
Then a horse broke. A longshot. Dog Day. They had to hold up the race. The gate men came around to get the horse and lead him back around to the back of the gate.


The young guys, the shirtless boys with the ladies’ voices came running down the steps.
“SHIT! WE STILL GOT TIME TO GET DOWN!”


They were agile, leaping two or three steps at a time. The last guy stumbled, lost his footing, rolled down the stairway.
“SHIT!!!!!”


He almost cracked his head open on a seat structure, leaped up, ran after the others.
“RED WINDOW!”
Who was reluctant to enter the gate.










129


3…


5-to-2…
They had to rump-push the horse in.
When the gate sprang Red Window read 9-to-5.
The race was a mile-and-one-sixteenth.


Red Window broke slowly from post 8. When he got clear the boy took him over to the rail to save ground. The field strung out around the first turn.


Thousands and thousands of dollars had gone in on Red Window in minutes, in seconds. It had to be good or the world was mad. Homes were on the line. Lives were on the line. Everything was on the line for this one. If there was ever any inside, this had to be inside!


Going down the backstretch Red Window had an opening on the rail. The boy let him out a bit and he moved up to 5th. He was just 4 lengths off the lead and a couple of speed horses were eating each other up in front. Red Window hugged the rail, holding his position, neither gaining nor falling back. Not good but not bad. If the horse was a powerhouse they were saving him for the one big move.
Around the final curve Red Window began to fall back.
Hey!


He was 6 lengths back, he was faltering, there was a wall of tiring horses, the sun fought with the smog, the mountains were a light purple, and Red Window appeared to be locked-in at the rail.
Then the boy went for it.
He pulled the horse out from the rail, circled the tiring horseflesh, he circled the field 4 wide and was really moving!
Red Window looked good.
Shit, thought Henry, this is it!
Red Window came wide into the stretch. He had clear sailing. He was a god damned locomotive. He was pounding and pumping, he was pumping and pounding. He looked twice as large as any other horse. He looked beautiful, so beautiful!
The miracle was unfolding.
The crowd screamed as one crazy voice. Life was good at last.


Then, the horse stopped running. He began climbing. He began to shake his head wildly.


The boy went crazy, he had been told the horse couldn’t miss, he slashed with the whip, half-rising in the saddle.
Red Window balked and slowed.








130


Then, he pulled up and stopped.


He refused to go to the finish line.
The morning line favorite crossed the line, reading 7-to-2 over a morning line of 8-to-5.
A chorus of boos and invectives came from the stands.


The horse stood there 70 yards from the finish line and refused to go on. The crowd was insane, and those near the rail were throwing beer cans at the horse, Racing Forms, hotdogs, dirt, anything they could find. One drunk took off both shoes and winged them. The roar and rattle of agony was everywhere. The clouds wavered and the mountains blinked.
Red Window stood there and excreted. He shit on the crowd.


Then done with that, he gathered himself, jolted forward, and with the boy with one foot out of the stirrups, Red Window began to run!


Red Window ran like a son of a bitch. He was full of energy. He looked magnificent. The boy yanked at the reins but he couldn’t hold him. The horse was as strong as ten thousand horses. He could run through a cement wall.
Red Window ran past the finish line, at last.


And then toward all the horses who had beaten him, the horses who were cantering in from the opposite direction.
The outriders took off after Red Window.


“LOOSE HORSE!” cried the announcer, “LOOSE HORSE ON THE RUN!” The jocks cleared a path and Red Window roared through.
The outriders finally corraled him on the backstretch.
Then he wouldn’t move. They yanked and whipped and cursed and ca-joled. Red Window stood, bolted to the ground.


They had to send the horse van. After a good four minutes they got him in there. He got a free ride back to the barn.
Then the parimutuel board flashed: $9.80 on the morning line favorite.
Henry tore up his ticket. His ticket joined all the others.
Well, he was only $110 down, not counting the 20 to Marsden. There was still time to pull it out. You only needed a couple of hits to get well.


“GOD,” one of the young guys in the back row yelled, “THIS SHIT FUCKING PLACE MAKES ME PUKE!”
Henry opened his program to the 4th race, one-and-one-sixteenth


















131
miles, dedicated to the Shamrock Meats Employees, purse $35,000. For four-year-olds and up, non-winners of two races of $3,000 other than maiden, claiming or starter, 120 pounds…
Maybe, Henry thought, I ought to try the stock market.
He took his program and his Form and walked down to the nearest bar. Never had he seen that bar so packed. The road to hell had plenty of com-pany but it was still so very lonely.
He pushed forward and elbowed in for his vodka-7.

146


THE JOCKEY




Warming up Blue Mongoose on the backstretch before the last race, Larry Peterson noticed that the horse was really rank, almost spooked. Larry had been riding for 15 years and he knew his horses. This one really had a bug up its ass.
Larry tried to ease the horse out of it but at post time things weren’t any better. He rode up to the gate ahead of the other horses and found McK-elvey. He told McKelvey, “This fucking beast is unfit. I want him scratched.” “He looks all right to me,” McKelvey answered. Larry knew that McK-elvey was one steward who worried about the money the track lost on a scratch. The money lost was negligible, however, because as soon as the
fools got their money back they bet it on something else.
Larry dismounted and gave the reins to McKelvey. “Get a feel of this motherfucker! See if you can hold him on the ground!”


McKelvey was a big fat guy. He grabbed the reins. Blue Mongoose bucked, tossed his head. The horse was in a lather.


“You son of a bitch, calm down!” MeKelvey yelled at the horse. He yanked at the reins and ran the horse in a circle, then in another and then another.
“McKelvey, you’re only making him worse!”


McKelvey pulled the horse up and glared at Peterson. “Nothing wrong with him, Larry! Either you mount up or I’m recommending they ground you 5 racing days for refusing to ride!”
“You’re taking the food out of my mouth, McKelvey.”
“Ride or starve, boy.”
“Shit!”


Larry mounted. The crowd, not knowing anything, applauded. Blue Mongoose was the 8 horse. They had the first seven in. Blue Mongoose wouldn’t enter his stall. Several of the gate men pushed at the horse’s rump until they got him in.


The beast was quivering and snorting. When they put the 9 horse into the stall next to him, that did it. Mongoose spooked. He reared high in the gate and dumped Larry loose and backwards, hard into the dirt. It was some shot but Larry was still conscious. He rolled over














147
slowly, got up. Then he walked around, limping, his right leg throbbing.


He was dizzy and he had bitten his tongue.
Larry spit out a mouthful of blood and there was the fat boy standing there looking at him. Larry said, “McKelvey, you son of a bitch, I hate every part of you!”


McKelvey gave the order and then the announcer came on over the public address system. “Ladies and Gentlemen, by order of the stewards, Blue Mongoose is scratched from this race. Your tickets will be refunded.”


Larry walked off the track and down through the tunnel.


A bad day, one 3rd place finish and four out of the money. One of them had been a 6-to-5 shot. Larry liked to run on or near the pace. Seemed like his agent never got him any early foot horses any more.


He got to the locker room, took off his tack. His valet was gone, the fucker had a hot date with a McDonald’s counter girl.


It was nice under the shower. Lance Griffith was standing a stall or two down. He’d finished 2nd in the feature race with a sixteen-to-one shot and was feeling pretty good.
“Hey, Larry!”
“Yeah?”
“Let’s go and get fucked tonight!”
“I’m a married man, Lance…”
“What the hell’s that got to do with it? I am too!” “I don’t play it that way.”


“Don’t be a jerk, Larry. While we’re riding our horses our old ladies are riding something else.”
“I don’t look at it like that.”


“You think they sleep with us because we scale in at 114 or because we’re
5 foot nothing? You’ve got some wising up to do, man.”
“Listen, I just got thrown. I’m hurt. I don’t want to listen to a lot of shit.” “O.K., Larry, O.K….”


The right leg had stiffened, and driving in was painful.


God damn McKelvey, worried about the track take. That track would be there long after all of them were gone.


The house was beautiful. It had cost $300,000 and there was almost no mortgage. Larry pulled into the drive, got the car into the garage, went up the steps to the door, opened it and Karina was on the telephone,








148
all lovely six feet of her. Larry was like most of the other jocks. He liked tall women. Long hair. College educations. Class.
“Reena, baby,” he said.


Karina glanced at Larry, waved an arm to motion him off. She was heavy into her phone call.


“Yeah, Mom, well, listen,” she said, “you should take better care of yourself. You need more friends. Oh, I can tell when you’re down…I know your voice. Listen, when are you coming to visit us? Everything’s lovely here…The trees are full of fruit, tangerines, oranges, lemons…Larry and I love your company! What? Oh, don’t be foolish! I mean it! Look, here’s Larry!”


Karina glanced at him, forcefully, and said in a quiet voice, “Say hello to Mama.”


Larry took the phone. “Hello, Stella…How you doing?…That’s good…Oh, I just got in…What? Oh, I’ve been riding…No, no winners today…Tomorrow maybe…Yes, oh yes, it’s warm out here…Well, look, you be good now…Here’s Karina…”


He handed the phone to his wife. Then he walked across the room and up the stairway. He went into the bathroom and let the hot water run into the tub. The leg was really getting stiff.


Larry walked to the bedroom, took off his shoes and stockings. Then, sitting on the bed, he tried to get out of his pants. The right leg had stiffened. The pain was immense. He could hardly get his pants off. Struggling with it, he laughed. It was so ridiculous. Then he had the pants off.


The undershirt and shorts were easier. He managed to get up. He took a few steps. The leg held up. He moved toward the bathroom. He got in there, bent over the tub, ran in some cold water and mixed it into the hot with his hand. He was bent over the tub like that when Karina walked in.
“I think you were a little rude to Mom…”


“Reena, I didn’t mean to be. I just couldn’t think of anything to say.” “You couldn’t? Well, you could try a little harder. Mother has feelings


just like everybody else! That woman has been through a lot, she’s a brave and wonderful person.”


Larry stood up, looked at the bathroom wall behind the tub. “Kid, I’m sure she is.”


“You really don’t mean that, you’re just saying it.” “Well, hell, I hardly know your mother.”














149


Larry managed to climb into the tub. The water seemed about right. He eased himself into the water. That hot water felt so good on the leg.
“You should make an effort to know her…”
Karina stood over him, so tall there, staring down at him. All that body.
Those graceful legs. Some filly. And she knew how to dress. Style, class.
Grooming.
That long hair. Red mixed into the gold. And natural. Those deep green eyes. Those eyes that could laugh. And those perfect teeth. Nice nose, nice chin. Neck a bit long. But a good mind. And she knew how to dress. She had on his favorite outfit, the dark blue dress that fit her just right.


“I said, ‘You should make an effort to get to know her!’” “Reena, I’m really beat…”


“Thinking of yourself. Always thinking of yourself, your god damned self!”
“God damned self?”
“Don’t you think there’s anybody else around here? Just you? The great jockey? And lately, the not-so-great jockey!”


“Reena, are you getting ready to have your period?” “No, are you? Are you about to have your period?”


Karina leaned over the tub, her hands resting on the edge, her eyes hard, her gold-red hair swirling down.
“Listen, babe, I’m sorry if…”
“Don’t babe me!”


Larry decided to give it up. There was nothing to say. Words would just lead to more ugliness.


Stealing a glance at her he saw her smile and he thought, ah, it’s going to get better, the whole thing was some kind of joke.
But it wasn’t that kind of smile.
And then it was gone. And he heard her again.
“Now you’re withdrawing! You don’t want to talk to me about it!” Larry splashed some water up under his chin, feeling quite foolish as he
did so.
“Look, Reena, let’s forget everything and start all over. Let’s have a drink and ease off. Things aren’t so bad.”


Karina leaned closer. “A drink? A drink, a drink, a drink, a drink…A little drink. That solves everything, doesn’t it?”
“It helps…”
“Can’t you face anything without a drink?”










150


He knew what she wanted to hear and so he said it. “No.”


Karina reached angrily into the water and scooped a handful into his face. “You asshole! You idiot asshole!”


Her tears were coming. He felt ill in his stomach. He wanted to be any-where but there. He wanted to be in jail, he wanted to be on skid row, he wanted to be lost in a desert, he wanted to be sucked away by quicksand.
“Just leave me alone,” he said.
Karina leaned closer. She no longer seemed so beautiful. “Leave you alone? Leave you alone? What for? So you can diddle with yourself? So you can play with yourself?”


“Yeah,” said Larry, “that. Let me have that.” “Oh oh…my god, that I’d end up with you!”
Larry looked at her. “I beg you, just get out of here and leave me alone!” “Why did I have to marry a miniature man,” she began, “I could have…” and then a flash of roaring red came over him, and then another, he grabbed her by the hair and by the neck and pulled her violently into the tub with
him.
There was a crash and the splash of legs and elbows, and she was in there, he was big enough to handle her, and he worked over on top of her as she kicked and flailed. He was accustomed to handling 1500 pounds of stubborn horse, he felt his fingers digging into her mouth, her nostrils, gripping her forehead, and he pushed down hard, hard, and her head went under and he held it there, he held it down there, thinking, she’s quiet now, but he couldn’t do it, he finally let her up, gasping and choking. He climbed out of the tub, ashamed. He grabbed a towel, and wrapped it about himself as Karina just sat there in the tub in her dark blue dress both of her hands up to her face. She just sat there like that.
Larry felt horrible, demented, worse than evil.
He walked into the bedroom, got into a robe. He sat in a chair by the bedroom window. Evening had gone into night. Below and to the east he could see the lights of the city. They looked very peaceful.


Then he heard Karina getting out of the tub. It made a splashing sound.
She coughed.
He heard her walking. He heard the water dripping as she walked. He felt her walking up behind him. He waited and looked at the lights of the city.
















151


the guillotine




I knew my black friend long ago when we both worked in the same pit of agony, now I see him occasionally in the grandstand at the track, he sits alone and works hard at the Racing Form (I long ago threw that away, noting that almost everybody had one and that
almost
everybody lost), anyhow, I saw him last Sunday: “hello, Roy…” “hello, Hank…” “I like the 9,” I said. “maybe” he said, “but there is one horse that can’t win…” he told me it was the 4 horse.


the 4 was reading three-to-one; I walked down and cancelled the 9 and
bought


a ticket on the 4, then I went out and watched the race. the 4 got up in the last jump and it did win.


which is my system: I listen to somebody badmouth a horse and I bet it. this is much better than any Racing Form.




I used to go to the track with a girlfriend and I’d see who she was betting in each race: if she bet the favorite, I’d bet the 2nd
favorite.


if she bet the 2nd favorite, I’d bet the favorite. if she bet the 3rd favorite, I’d bet the 2nd favorite.


during all the time I went to the track with her I only lost once.
she never won.


it pissed her off so much that she quit seeing me.


this Monday, a holiday, St. Patrick’s Day, I was in line behind a little


old lady dressed in green; it was a 6 horse field and I listened to her place her bets: she wanted 2 win on the one horse, 2 place on the
2 horse,








152


2 show on the 4 horse, 2 show on the 5 horse and 2 win on the 6 horse.
she left out the 3 and the 3 was a longshot, 11-to-one.


I bet 5 win on the 3 and the 3 won by two-and-one-half lengths.


you might call me a vulture, feasting on the brains of losers but it doesn’t cost them anything extra and they’re going to lose anyhow


anybody can lose, it doesn’t take any art to lose and it isn’t a brave thing to do, it’s dumb


I used to know this fellow who would storm about my apartment:


“shit,
I lost again! every fucking cent!”


he’d have every kind of alibi for his losses but nothing changes the end-result.


“sit down,” I’d tell him, “and have a drink…”


then I’d pour him one from my expensive 5th of Scotch and he’d sit there,


hair in his eyes, stinking in his dirty socks, his shirt with buttons missing here and there
and he’d slam his drink down


as disgusted with the living and the dead as I was.

161


CAMUS




Larry awakened, got out of the twisted sheets, walked to the window which overlooked the neighborhood to the east. He saw the garage roofs and the trees with their barren branches. His hangover was about standard and he walked to the bathroom to piss, did that, turned to the basin to wash his hands, then he splashed water on his face. Then he did it: he looked at the face in the mirror, found it less than enchanting. He let the bathwater run, thinking, the problem with the History of Man is that it doesn’t lead any-where except toward certain death for the individual, and that was drab and ugly, garbage disposal stuff.
His cat, Hog, walked in. Hog just stared at him, he wanted his cat food. That animal, thought Larry, is just a walking belly, and if I want to fly back east for a couple of weeks I’ve either got to board the son of a bitch or shoot him. Maybe if I want to fly back east I ought to shoot myself; but I don’t want to shoot myself. Too many men have been shot, I want something more individual. Like pills? No, pills were too blase, even when they in-duced death.
Larry checked his face in the glass again: shave? No.


Larry made it to his 11 a.m. class.


There they were: those young girls, the promise that never lasted, those young girls, those great momentary decorations, so bright, so fresh. He liked them. The boys were almost as good as the girls. As the decades rolled on the boys and the girls were becoming almost one. The boys had a grace that the boys of his age had never had. They also seemed more kind. One thing they seemed to lack was courage but maybe their courage was more sublime, hidden. The Atomic Generation had bred a strange gang, and Larry had decided long ago that to judge them was only a protective shield raised to hide his own shortcomings.


Larry looked at them from behind his desk. That desk, the symbol of power.
“Well, shit…” he said.
Some of them laughed.
“I’ve already shit,” some bright guy said.
“Did you wipe?” Larry asked.












162


“Probably not enough,” the bright guy responded.


“Which is the answer to almost everything,” Larry suggested.
“Hey,” said a fat boy in a yellow jumpsuit from one of the rear seats, “all this talk about shit. I thought this was a course in Modern Lit. Is this what they pay you for?”
“Most men are terribly incompetent in their profession. I might be one of those. I’m not quite sure. One thing I am quite sure of is that I can kick your ass. This isn’t really important but somehow it soothes me…”
The kid in the yellow jumpsuit leaped up: “I’ll call you on that!” “O.K.,” said Larry, “let’s go.”


The class filed slowly outside. They waited for Larry and the boy. They formed a circle under the oak tree near the library. The warriors arrived. Larry took off his coat, threw it on the ground. The fat boy in the jumpsuit inhaled deeply and puffed himself up. He looked like several thousand frogs. Then he charged.


Larry jabbed him coming in, then dug a right into his gut. The fat boy let out a little fart, backed off.
Then the fat boy began circling. Larry began circling.
They both circled. They circled and circled.
“Come on!” somebody in the crowd hollered. “Let’s get it on!” Larry waved the fat boy in: “Come on, I’ll cut you to pieces!” “You old fuck,” said the fat boy, “I’ll kick your dead ass!”
They kept circling. Some of the students returned to the classroom for their belongings. Others left for somewhere else.
Then Larry and the boy were alone, circling.
The fat boy said, “I’m gonna get my dad to have you fired!”
“We aren’t going to fight,” said Larry, “we are afraid of each other.” Larry turned and walked back to the classroom. When he got there about
half the class was waiting.


Then the fat boy walked in and took his seat in the rear. Larry looked at him: “You’re going to have a tough time getting an ‘A’ out of me.”


“I know,” the boy answered. “That takes tight young pussy.” “And more than once,” Larry added. Larry surveyed what was left of the class:


“Now, anybody else who wants the shit kicked out of them, please stand up!”
One of the boys stood up. Then another. Soon they were all standing.
Then one of the girls stood up. Then another. Soon everybody was standing.










163


“All right,” said Larry, “sit down. Or I’m going to flunk this whole fucking class.”
They sat down.


“Power destroys,” Larry told them, “and the lack of it creates a world of misfits. But I’ll let you off the hook. I won’t flunk you if one of you can name a fairly good writer for me. His name spelled backwards is ‘s-u-m-a-c.’”
“Smack,” said some wise guy.


“No. that’s ‘Kcams,’ the great Hungarian poet and horse thief of the 19th century. You’ve all just flunked. What do you think about that?”
“What do you think about Capote?” somebody asked.
“I never think about him.”
“Mailer?”
“Just his wives.”
“God?”
“I especially don’t think about God.”


“If you especially don’t,” said somebody, “that means that you especially do.”


“You mean,” asked Larry, “that if I don’t fuck it means that I do?” Then the bell rang, tolling for everybody.


That seemed more like 20 minutes, Larry thought. Nothing like a bit of brisk physical exercise to pass the time.


“When I see you next Wednesday, if I do,” Larry addressed the departing students, “I’ll expect an essay from each of you, the subject will be ‘Who Wrote Our National Anthem, and Why?’”


They filed out, grumbling profanities like what has this got to do with Modern Lit.?


Then they were gone except for one young girl who closed in on Larry’s desk.


She looked very fine in the noon light. It drove through her thin tight dress. He sat there. He felt her flank rub against his left shoulder.


“I like you, Jansen,” she said, using his last name, “I don’t know how to say this, it might sound awkward…”
“Just press your legs together tight and try.”


“Well, I understand why your class is the most popular on campus. It’s energetic, descriptive, it’s entertaining, it’s got soul…”


“Soul, that’s what we need. Thank you…” “Denise.”
“Thank you, Denise.”








164


She pressed her flank against him. “This is a bit easier for me to say: if you ever want some of that tight young pussy you’re always talking about…”
“You don’t mean that?” he looked up at her.
“Sure, for that ‘A,’ I mean it.”
Larry kept looking at her. “Jesus Christ, do you think I can be bought that easily?”


“Yes,” she smiled, “just write your phone number on that note pad there, rip it off and give it to me. I’ll arrange the rest…”


Larry picked up his pen and wrote his number down, slid it toward her flank. Her hand came down, picked up the paper, folded it, and then she was gone.


Larry stood up, put his coat on. He had a 2 p.m. class and then the day was over.


One thing he knew, though, he was going to flunk that fat son of a bitch in the yellow jumpsuit. And wasn’t that something? Arthur Koestler and his wife in a double suicide?


He walked out of the class and was soon upon the campus green. Time for a quiet lunch at the Blue Moon and a couple of drinks. It was a mile or so from the university but well worth the drive. A damn good place to unwind.

FAME




John Marlowe and his agent David Hudson had been taking turns at the wheel. John was driving as they came out of the low hills and saw the long flat road ahead that looked endless.


Dave lit a cigarette. “Shit, if we hadda been flying we’d be laying up in New York now calling room service for drinks…”


“I’m sorry, Dave, but I just can’t face another plane trip like that last one.”
“What happened?”


“Well, I didn’t have any trouble at the airport and I thought it was going to be a good trip and everything was all right until we got up there and the stewardess came by taking orders for drinks.”
“Yeah?”
“Yeah. I ordered and she kind of blinked, swayed a little. Then she got
entirely unprofessional and in this loud voice she asked, ‘You’re John


Marlowe, aren’t you?’”
“What happened?”


“What happened? The trip was ruined! People came around for auto-graphs, they turned around in their seats and stared at me.”
“Is that so bad, John? I wouldn’t mind.”


“Listen, Dave, you’d mind after a while. You can never be yourself, you can never feel at ease, you can never relax.”


“You wanted to be an actor. Just five years ago nobody knew who you were.”


“I like to act, it’s all I know how to do. But you find out there’s nothing like privacy. Until you lose it you don’t realize what you had.”


“Shit, man, look at the money you’re making! Suffer a little!” John Marlowe laughed, “You’re right, sure. But Jesus Christ…”


They drove along in silence for a while. At least there’s nobody out here, thought John. Just jackrabbits, snake and brush.


“On that plane,” said John, “the worst thing was that they showed one of my films. Think of the odds against that! Here I’m on the plane and they’ve got me in the can! My worst movie, ‘The Hit Man,’ I had to watch it with them. When it was over everybody on the plane applauded.”














180


The agent ground his cigarette out in the tray. “You’re a famous man, John, you’re a successful man. You know the saying, ‘It comes with the territory.’”


“Yeah, but it still feels good to be driving along here without anybody around.”
“Hey, John, I’m here!”
“I didn’t mean it that way. I mean, you’re used to me. I can relax around you. Shit, man, you don’t know what it is when you can’t go into a super-market or go get your driver’s license renewed or go out to eat without being recognized. You can’t do any of the common little things that the average man does. It’s hell.”


The actor pulled the car over to the side of the road and kicked the door open. “You drive a while, Dave.”


Dave slid over to the wheel and the actor got in on the other side. They moved on again.


“You can’t even have a real girlfriend, Dave. You figure she’s just with you because you’re famous.”
“I’d fuck ’em all, John.”


“That wears thin damned fast. You want something real.” “We all want that but few of us get it.”


“You’re right. And I’m bitching too much, I know. But the strangest thing about being famous is that you don’t feel famous. You feel like you always did. It’s just in the eyes of the public that you’re famous. The whole thing is like a dream, if you know what I mean…”
“God damn it, you take my job and I’ll take yours!”
“Dave, I wouldn’t mind, I wouldn’t mind at all except you’re the better agent and I’m the better actor and we’re stuck with that.”


“Hey, look,” said Dave, “I see an eating place up ahead. Let’s stop. I can use a beer and a burger.”
“O.K.,” said John.


They pulled in and parked, got out and walked into the cafe, Louie’s Haven. It was about 2 p.m. The place was dirty and empty, just the waitress and the fry cook. They sat in the only booth and waited. The waitress glanced over but kept talking to the fry cook. She knew there wasn’t another cafe for twenty more miles.


“This is nice,” said the actor, “it’s quiet.” “Yeah, but my mouth’s watering for a beer.”


“Listen, Dave, why do we have to make this fucking trip, anyhow?” “It’s in the contract. You signed the contract. Personal appearance,








181
New York City. They are going to interview you on ‘Good Morning, New York’ about your new movie. Great publicity.”
“Shit.”
“Well, you signed the contract. Hey, here she comes!”
The waitress was a big girl in a pink dress, about 22, she chewed gum, wore white tennis shoes. Across her apron were the words: I SERVE IT HOT AND READY! She stopped at the table and looked out the window as if there was something out there.
“I’m Eva,” she said, “we don’t have a menu. It’s on the board.”
There was a blackboard on the wall. It said:
  



Chicken Soup
Chili
Pie
Sandwiches
Tamales
Beer


“I’ll have,” said the agent, “a burger with onions and a beer. I’ll have the beer now, please.”
The big girl just kept looking out the window.
“I’ll have a roast beef sandwich and coffee,” said the actor.
The waitress stood a while longer staring out the window. Then she whirled as if angry and they watched her big haunches move up and down under her pink dress. She said something to the fry cook and then after a wait that seemed much longer than necessary she came walking back to-ward the booth with a beer bottle topped with a glass and a cup of coffee on a saucer. She slammed the beer down in front of Dave, then looked at John Marlowe for the first time.


She blinked several times, she stopped chewing her gum. She stood there holding the coffee cup on the saucer. Her hand began to tremble. The cup rattled and bounced on the saucer. Black coffee jumped out of the cup.
“Oooh…oooh, you’re John Marlowe, ain’t ya?”


“Yes, I guess I am. And this is my friend, Dave Hudson.” “Pleased to meet you, mam,” said Dave Hudson. “JOHN MARLOWE!”














182


She managed to set the coffee cup down. Her mouth opened into a little circular hole. Her eyes were little circular holes. She inhaled, exhaled.
“Oh my god!” she spoke. “I almost shit myself!”
“Listen,” said the actor, “relax. And bring me a beer too, please.”
The waitress ran off. Then she was back like a shot with the beer and a pen. She put the beer down and pulled a napkin from the table dispenser.


“Would you please give me your autograph, Mr. Marlowe? And say ‘to Eva,’ ‘Eva Evans’!”


Then they waited for their order. The waitress stood beside the fry cook. The fry cook stared and stared. A mass of smoke arose. He had burned the burger. He threw on another. Then the waitress came out around the counter and walked quickly to the wall telephone. They watched her dial several numbers and whisper excitedly into the phone.


“This is the beginning,” the actor said to the agent, “this is what I was talking about.”
He picked up his beer and drained half of it.


“I’m hungry,” said the agent, “I’ve got to have that burger. We can grab our food and take off.”


“One thing,” said the actor, “there can’t be too many fucking people around this part of the country. I didn’t see a house or a farm for miles, did you?”
“No.”
The waitress finished on the telephone and stood very near their table. “Them sandwiches are coming up in a minute!”
“Just make them as fast as possible, please,” said John Marlowe.


“Yes, sir! And Louie said, ‘no charge.’ We are honored! Oh Lord, I might shit myself yet!”
“Please, lady,” said John Marlowe, “don’t.”


“I saw in your last picture where you gave up everything to live with the Indian girl on the reservation!” said the waitress.


“Wasn’t that an old John Wayne movie?” “No, Mr. Marlowe, that was YOU!”


Then after what seemed a long wait they heard it: the sound of engines. Then they saw them: a motorcycle, a pick-up, a couple of beat-up vans, all Fords, Chevvies, not a foreign car in the bunch, good country folk, raising the dust in the parking lot and then the screen
















183
door of the cafe slapped open and soon all the stools at the counter were taken, mostly by men but there were two or three women and they all sat there, sometimes looking straight forward, other times just spinning casually on their stools and staring.
“These are probably nice people in their way,” thought John Marlowe, “but they make me nervous.”
More and more people just turned on their stools and stared.


“Holy Christ,” said the agent, “I think I’m beginning to know what you mean.”
“Think of biting into a sandwich with all those eyes watching.”
“I’m not hungry. Let’s go!”
“I’m all for it, Dave.”
Marlowe dropped a twenty on the table and they both got up.


“HEY, WAIT! YOUR SANDWICHES ARE ALMOST READY NOW!” the waitress yelled.

“Fuck it. This is a 1988 Mercedes. See what she can do!” “My pleasure.”


The actor put it to the floor. The car responded nicely. Behind them there was the stream of vehicles and a billowing cloud of dust and exhaust. Quickly they began to leave them behind. Except for one. The motorcycle. The bike hung in grimly.
“What does the son of a bitch want?” asked Dave.
“I don’t know. But he looks like he means it.”
“He’s insane.”
“Yes.”


The biker trailed them for some ten miles. They could neither gain on him nor he on them. It was unsettling.


“The son of a bitch has watched too many movies,” said John Marlowe. “It has eaten his brain away.”
“Yeah,” said Dave Hudson.


It was then that they hit a curve in the road covered with gravel and sand. The car skidded but the motorcycle flipped sideways and left










184
the ground, throwing the rider off. There was something very final about it.


“He’s finished,” said the actor, “the son of a bitch is gone.” “Right,” said the agent, “on to New York!” “On to New York!” said John Marlowe.


“I never knew you could drive so well,” said Dave, “you can really tool this son of a bitch.”
“Thanks.”
“Hey, where the hell are you going?”

“We can’t leave him there like that. He might be badly hurt.”
“John, he’s just a crazed fan.”
“Even a crazed fan deserves mercy.”
They drove back. Then they spotted the bike and stopped.
“There he is,” said Dave. They got out of the car and walked over.
The biker was over on the side of the road hung up in a clump of brush. One arm dangled oddly. It was broken. The left arm. A point of bone poked out with just the faintest smear of blood on it. The man was conscious. His eyes were open.
“You all right?” Dave asked.
“No, I’m not, fucker,” he said.


John went to the car and opened the trunk. There was a blanket in there. They lifted the cursing biker gently out of the brush, lay him on the


ground and covered him with the blanket. He seemed unable to move. “You’ll be all right,” said the actor. “Dave, you go back to the cafe and

“John Marlowe,” the biker said from under the blanket, “you’re not such a great man!”

“How do you know? He might have internal injuries.”
“John Marlowe,” said the biker, “you’re not such a great man.”
His right hand came up from under the blanket with the pistol. He pointed it at John Marlowe. For a moment everybody was motionless. Then the biker fired. The bullet hit John Marlowe square in the face, just above the nose. John Marlowe fell across the body of the biker.
Dave somehow managed to grab the gun away, threw it as far as












185
he could into a field to one side of the road, pulled the actor off the biker, saw death, and found it quite unpleasant. He turned, walked a few paces and vomited. It had all happened too quickly. It wasn’t possible.


He turned back to the biker who lay quietly under the blanket and was watching him. John Marlowe lay beside him. For some reason Dave didn’t feel any fear. He wanted to kill the biker. Everything seemed unreal and out of focus.


Finally he asked in a shaky voice, “What did you do that for?” “I don’t know. I guess he was a great man. I loved him.” “That was a hell of a way to show it.”
“It’s like, this way I’m a part of him, forever.” “You’re a fucking maniac!”
Dave turned and walked toward the car.


“Hey, man, take me with you! I need a doctor! I can’t move! This arm really hurts!”


Dave stopped, looked at the man, then got in the car. The key was in the ignition. He started the car and drove back toward Louie’s Haven.


Dave had always wanted a new Mercedes. Now he had one. For a while anyhow.

my friend, Howie




had worse luck with women than most, finally married, they had a baby and it went all right for 2 or 3 years then all at once

“where have you been?” he asked her and she said, “he shows me his

I was sitting on a barstool in the “8-Count,” not particularly thinking about anything, like what was I doing there drinking scotch and water. Maybe it was because Marie was bitching because I wanted to take flying lessons. But she was always bitching about something. I waved the bartender in for a new scotch and water for me and a beer for the old fellow. He was drinking draft beer. Maybe that’s what had made his eyes come so close together: green, cheap beer.

He smiled.

Monty was depressed, well, not depressed, just discouraged with the entire set-up, the entire game, the entire life. THE WRITERS




Harold knocked at the apartment door.


Nelson was at the kitchen table eating a slice of cheesecake and having a cup of espresso.


“Yes?” Nelson asked. Knocks at the door made him nervous. And when he became nervous he developed a head-tic. His head started bobbing.
“Who is it?”
“Nelson, it’s Harold.”
“Oh, just a moment…”
Nelson picked up the remainder of the cheesecake and jammed it into his mouth. As he chewed at the cake his eyes watered. He was 45 pounds overweight. He ingested the last gulp, hurried to the sink, ran water over the plate, over his hands, then walked to the door, undid the chain, twisted the knob, opened the door.


Harold entered. He was 5 feet tall and thin. He was 68. Nelson was some 30 years younger. They both were writers but they only wrote poetry. Their books sold sparingly and how they survived was kept secret. They each had small channels of furtive income from somewhere. But neither of them ever spoke about this.
“Care for some espresso?” Nelson asked.
“Oh, well, yes…”
Harold sat down. Nelson soon brought him his cup. Then Nelson sat beside him on the couch by the coffeetable.
Nelson’s head began to bob and shake again.


“Well, Harold, I saw the son of a bitch. He granted me a visit.” Harold lifted his cup halfway to his mouth, stopped. “Fucktowski?” he asked.
That was their name for this writer.
“Yeah.”

BLOCKED




It was 11:45 a.m. when the phone rang. Martin Glisson was asleep. He picked the phone up off the floor.
“Yeah?” he asked.
“Martin?”
“Yeah.”
“This is The Rodent.”


It was the editor of a New York magazine who liked to call himself The Rodent.


“Listen, we don’t have anything from you. It’s six days before deadline.” “O.K., Rodent, I’ll get something to you.”


Martin wrote a short story every month for the magazine Sexerox. “How are you doing with the ladies, Martin?”


“I’m giving myself a break. I’m staying away from them.” “Where do you get your material?”
“What does it matter as long as it reads all right?”


“You’re right. We like your stuff. For all we know, you might be a virgin. Anyhow, we need something in six days.”
“All right, Rodent. Hang in.”
“Sure, Martin…”
Martin dropped the phone in the cradle. He rolled back into the bed, belly down, his face looking east into the sun. The booze was sweating out of him. He’d written 27 books, been translated into 7 or 8 languages and he’d never had a writer’s block and now he had a fucking writer’s block.


He stared into the sun. He had only escaped the 8 hour job 13 years ago. Now, all of TIME was his. Every second, every minute, every hour, every day. Every night. He was a writer. A writer. A writer. A professional writer. There were 12 million people in America who wanted to be writers. He was a writer.


Martin got out of bed and went to the bathroom, let the water run in the tub, then went over and sat on the toilet. He knew his problem: he couldn’t get to the typewriter. It was in the other room. All he had to do was walk in there and sit down at the typewriter and it would
















244
come. But he couldn’t do that. He’d walk in there, look at the machine, but he wouldn’t sit down. He couldn’t. And he didn’t know exactly why.
Well, at least he could excrete.


Martin wiped himself, looked down, flushed it away, thinking, there’s a very thin line between writing and excreting.
He walked to the tub, mixed in some cold water, then climbed in….


Writing pushed you into airy spaces, made you strange, a misfit. No wonder Hemingway blew his brains out over the orange juice. No wonder Hart Crane jumped into the propeller, no wonder Chatterton took rat poison. The only ones who continued were the ones who wrote best-sellers and they weren’t writing, they were already dead. And maybe he was dead too: he owned his own house with a security system, he had an IBM electric typewriter, he had a Porsche and a BMW in his garage. But so far he had resisted the swimming pool, the jacuzzi and the tennis court. Maybe he was only half-dead?
The phone rang. He smiled: get in the tub and the phone will ring. The phone used to ring while he was fucking. It didn’t anymore. He was a writer, he couldn’t bother with fucking. He needed the time to write sex stories.


He got out of the tub, wet and dripping, made it to the bedroom, picked up the phone.
“Yes?”
“Martin Glisson?”
“Yes.”


“This is Dr. Warner’s office. I want to remind you that you have a one o’clock appointment.”
“Holy shit!”
“What?”
“I mean, what for?”


“It’s your semi-annual appointment to get your teeth checked and cleaned.”
“All right, thank you…”


Martin didn’t get back into the tub. He just walked to the bedroom, fell on the bed and rolled around on the sheets to dry off. He still had some originality.


Then he got dressed and went outside. He looked at the two cars, then chose the BMW. He felt the need for a little change.












245


Later, at the dentist’s office, he notified the receptionist that he was there. The girl told him to please be seated, then slid the glass partition shut. He never liked it when they slid that glass partition shut. It was really an af-front, shutting one out like that. Or maybe they didn’t want you to hear the screaming from the dentist’s chair. No matter.
Martin walked over and sat down, picked up a magazine.


What he liked about Sexerox was that they published everything he sent them. He should really try to write something now, just to keep that avenue open. Maybe he didn’t have writer’s block. Maybe he just thought he had writer’s block. But the end result was the same.


He had forgotten his reading glasses. Still, he turned the pages of the magazine. He couldn’t read magazines anyhow even with his glasses. He wasn’t interested in sports, world affairs, movies, the stage, royalty, or even whether the world ended or not.
“Hi, mister!”


It was a little girl, about 5, dressed in a cute blue dress, white shoes. She was blonde with a red ribbon in her hair. She had large beautiful brown eyes.
“Hi!” Martin answered, then looked down at his magazine again.
“You gonna get your teeth pulled?” the little girl asked.
Martin looked up again.
“Gee, I don’t know. I hope not.”
Martin looked at her again. She was really a cute little thing. But she’d probably grow up to be a ball-buster.
“You got a funny face,” she said.
Martin smiled.
“You got a funny face too.”


She laughed. It was a great little laugh, chilly and neat, reminded him of ice cubes in the bottom of a glass. No, that was lousy. The laugh was something else. What?


That’s it, there’s one, thought Martin: man molests little girl in dentist’s waiting room while her mother is getting a wisdom tooth extracted. And make it realistic and terrible, yet humorous. Man wants to but doesn’t want to, yet in her way little girl leads him on. When mother walks out he has little girl’s panties on his head.
“Where’s your mother?” Martin asked the little girl.
“She’s getting a tooth pulled.”
“Oh…”
Martin looked back down at his magazine.

“Just read to me from that magazine you’re holding.”

NO LOVE SONGS




Dear Editor:


I realize I missed the deadline but I’ve been beset by trivialities, like ar-guments with the female, car breakdowns, a house guest for one week, and various other things I can’t remember. One I can remember is that I had to get my driver’s license renewed. Each time I get my driver’s license renewed I begin to realize how much older I am, it’s a sign that you’re moving along toward the grave, a more telling sign than New Year’s Eve or birthdays, and although I really don’t mind dying I do dislike the cer-tainty of it. So every four years at driver’s license renewal time I decide to go on one huge drunk. So, having done that, I was driving along the next day, toward the Hollywood Dept. of Motor Vehicles but my head hurt too much to face it. And I kept blacking out. So I took a right, found a bar up near Hollywood Boulevard, I think I was on Las Palmas or Cherokee, parked, got out, went in, sat down, got a Heineken from the barkeep, no glass, and took a good haul…


A couple of stools down sat an old gal who looked like she had porcupine bristles for hair. And it looked like she had cut a hole in the center of a bedsheet, a dirty bedsheet, slipped her head through and put the damn thing on.
“Hey,” she said.
I looked at her more directly.
“I’m Helena the Gypsy,” she said.
“Phillip Messbell, unemployed air traffic controller,” I answered.
“Read your palm, Phillip?”
“How much?”
“A beer.”
“O.K.”
Helena dragged her Klan sheet over to the stool next to mine, grabbed my left hand, twisted it and began to finger my palm.


“Ah,” she said, “you have a long life line. You will live a long time…” “That’s already been done. Tell me something new.”
“Ah,” she rubbed some more, “Heineken is your favorite beer.”
















258


“I said cut the crap.”


“Oh, now I see it!” she exclaimed.
“Yeah? What?”
“You’re going to get fucked within the next hour.”
“Who? By you?”
“Maybe. You got $25?”
“No.”
“Not by me…”


She got her beer, I finished mine and got out of there. I got into the car and took a left at the boulevard, toward the DMV. My head felt a little better, but I was still hazy. I’d have to pass the frigging test without having read the instruction book, but that was all right. What I hated was standing in the long lines and looking at the backs of heads. The backs of heads didn’t look as bad as the fronts but nevertheless it was horrifying. I drove on…


As I pulled up at the next signal I suddenly felt the need to excrete. In order to keep my mind off that I glanced about the boulevard. I saw this young woman sitting on the bus stop bench, might have been M. Monroe come back to life, only a little more beat-up. And with fuller flanks and certainly a more lascivious leer. I smiled at her pulled-back skirt which showed me so much more than I had seen in months and she saw me looking and smiled back. I was smiling. She was smiling. It was a smiling world. Just as the light turned green she jumped up and ran toward my car. I kicked out my right leg, the door flew open and she slithered in like a grapevine ready to be plucked.
The guy in the car behind me honked and yelled, “IF THAT WHORE DON’T
KILL YOU, NOTHING EVER WILL!”

“My name’s Rosie,” she said.
“Gordon Plugg,” I told her.
“You want Dip Down,” asked Rosie, “or Around the World? You want Epsom Salts, Brown Dog or the Yellow Sea? The Wire Whip? Suction Cup? The Broom Handle? I do Three Hands Mary and the Chimney Sweep. What do you want?”
“I want to renew my driver’s license,” I told her.
“That’ll be 50 bucks.”

this horseshoe

LONELY AT THE TOP




Marty rang the doorbell and waited and the door opened and a big guy let him in and he followed the big guy in, down a long hallway and at the end of the hallway was another room and the big guy opened the door and Marty walked in and there sat Kasemeyer behind the desk and Kasemeyer said, “Sit down.” Marty sat down in a chair in front of the desk and the big guy closed the door and was gone, but not very far. Kasemeyer didn’t look like much but he was everything (if anything was everything) not only in that city but in many cities and some countries too.
“Marty,” Kasemeyer asked, “how long you been a hit man?”


“A long time, sir, and I’ve never been nailed. I’ve always found a pigeon to take the rap.”
“Who have been some of your hits?”

“Didn’t you burn Huey Long?”


“I don’t go back that far, sir. That hit was by my father.” “You come from a hell of a family, Marty.” “Thank you, sir.”

it into his mouth.

It was a press conference at the office of the Groundhogs. The cameras flashed and the owner had his arm around the shoulders of the new man-ager. Only he wasn’t new. Clint Stockmeyer had hired Larry Nelson twice before and had fired him twice. Stockmeyer was a big man, big of chest and paunch and money. The weight of him almost bent Larry over but Larry flashed a weak smile in comparison to Stockmeyer’s vast grin.


“How long is he gonna last?” one of the reporters asked Stockmeyer. “Well, we’ve given Larry a two year contract just to reflect our confidence


in him. We consider Larry the best manager in the league.” “If he was so good, why did you can him twice?”


There was some laughter. Even Larry Nelson laughed weakly as he slid out from under Stockmeyer’s arm.

“It seems to me, Mr. Stockmeyer, that you’re doing all the talking. What does Larry have to say?”


Larry slid out from under Stockmeyer’s arm again and stepped a little bit forward.
