THE WINNER




It was the 6th round and Tony Musso was landing tough body shots on Bobby Barker. Bobby had big red welts on both sides of his body. Each time he took another shot you could see he wanted to get out of the ring but he really didn’t know how. It could be done: I was in Philly once when Red Dog Jensen had had enough—he just stepped through the ropes and was gone.


It hurt me every time Bobby took another shot. Musso was a bull, a sadist. His mind was a golf ball but he could HIT. Musso would never be champ but he’d make a lot of guys quit the profession. Bobby was too worried about his face. He liked to dance and stick out his jab and look good for the girls at ringside. Once in a while he would try a little flurry, his punches were sharp and quick but he really didn’t have the power. Between fights we tried to keep him away from the girls, the parties, and the coke, but he slipped away a lot. Too bad. He was a nice boy in a lot of ways.


Bobby took a hard left hook to the ribs, grinned, then the bell rang. We got the stool out, he sat down and we got the mouthpiece out,


sponged him, put the water bottle to his mouth, let him rinse and spit, put the ice pack on the back of his neck. Then before I could say anything he said, “Fuck you…I know: stick and move…stick and move…move in, land a combination, move out. Fuck you.”


“Don’t talk,” I said. “Listen. You don’t have to take any more. Next time he lands one on your ribs, drop, take the count.”
“What the hell kind of a manager are you?” Bobby asked.


“I’m just trying to save your ass. There’s no way you’re going to beat Musso. Listen, we’ll go to Mexico City and start all over again.”
“Bullshit, I’m not fighting any Mexicans. They fight to kill.”


The warning buzzer sounded. Buzzard, our second, put Bobby’s mouthpiece in. We got the stool and bucket out of there, the bell rang and Musso charged toward Bobby. Bobby stuck him with a left and slipped to one side. Bobby had 7 straight wins but he had never been in with anybody like Musso. Musso was 22-7-1 and had been in with the best. Bobby’s wins had all been 6 rounders, this was his first 10 round fight and it was on na-tional tv. Pretty Boy Bobby vs. Musso the














339
Italian Bull. I had moved Bobby up too fast. It would be a nice purse, most probably his last good one. But I had a kid in the gym, Rickey Munson, he loved to fight, didn’t chase women, all he liked to do was watch tv. I had hopes for him.
It was hot in there and I drank a swallow or two out of the water bottle.
There was plenty for me and Bobby.
“Bobby can’t handle Musso,” Buzzard said.
“Well, there’s nothing we can do,” I said.


Just then Musso landed a powerful left hook to Bobby’s ribs. You could hear it all over the arena. Bobby grinned, bent forward a little, stuck out a weak left jab, backed off. Musso followed. Boxing wasn’t always a pleasant profession but there weren’t many pleasant professions. You take a lawyer, the pay’s good but what a lot of slime. And how about a dentist? A person’s mouth was uglier than their asshole. Take a garage mechanic. Banged-up hands, grease that will never wash off, and you’ve got to jack up the charges one way or another just to get by. Besides, people are complete assholes when it comes to their cars. A zoo keeper? He has to hose out cages all day and answer questions like, “Do giraffes sleep?” There weren’t many pleasant professions but boxing could get ugly. A fight could get pretty one-sided before they stopped it. As long as you fought back now and then, even if you had no chance, they usually let it go on. And the fans, they wanted to see one guy or the other dead. That’s what they really wanted.


I heard some screaming, looked up: Bobby was down. The referee pushed Musso toward a neutral corner and began counting.


“Get up, Bobby!” some doll screamed from the first row. What did she know? The hardest thing she ever did was get a sun tan. She looked like a hooker. Now, there was a profession. Only problem was it didn’t last long. You had to score fast and big.


Bobby, the asshole, got up at 8. The referee wiped his gloves, looked at his eyes. Bobby nodded “yes” at the ref and the ref waved the fight on. Musso charged in and Bobby ducked under a vicious right as the bell rang.


We had the stool ready. Buzzard got the mouthpiece out. I ran some water down Bobby’s trunks, then put the bottle to his mouth.


“You jerk,” I told him, “what did you get up for?” “I’m going to beat him, Harry,” he said.


“This is no ‘Rocky’ film, Bobby. It just doesn’t work that way. That’s fantasy bullshit. All you want to do now is to get out of here














340
alive! You’ve probably got a couple of busted ribs right now. Next time you go down, stay down! The fans don’t give a fuck about you. You’ve got to care about yourself!”


“Jesus, Harry, you’re supposed to be my manager, you’re supposed to tell me how to beat this guy! Harry, you’re a lousy fucking manager.”


“I’m trying to save your ass, Bobby, and you ought to appreciate that. I’m not treating you like a hunk of meat.”


The buzzer sounded and we cleared the stuff out of there. Then the bell rang.
“Harry,” Buzzard said, “you told him right.”


Round 8. Musso came running out of his corner. Now, there was a guy whose contract I’d love to hold. Durable. Dumb. He even lived with his mother and donated a part of his purse to a Catholic orphanage. Women? Hell, he thought women were something you married. He didn’t drink. He didn’t smoke. Drugs were something he read about. All he did was cave in fighters. He was a lovely, lovely sight…


But I noticed he was slowing down. He seemed to hesitate before he stepped in. He still looked good, though, compared to Bobby, who had those red welts on his ribs and had lost all 7 rounds. Bobby blew a kiss to one of the dolls at ringside. She screamed like a soprano drunk on a pint of brandy.


Bobby jabbed and moved off. Then, he moved in, shot 3 lefts and 2 rights, to the head and body. Then, he moved out. He did a little shuffle. Musso charged in. Bobby threw his first right hand lead of the fight. It caught Musso by surprise. He stopped, blinked. It had caught him square on the nose. The blood came from his nostrils. Musso rubbed his nose with his glove and Bobby caught him with an uppercut as he moved in. Few fighters use the uppercut but there is nothing wrong with it. This one came from the floor and had great leverage. Musso backed up a few steps. Bobby moved in with a one-two to the jaw. Musso just stood there, trying to hold his hands up. He stood there looking at Bobby.


Then Bobby reached down into his shoes and pulled the power out of them, the power that had been missing all night. He cracked away with rights and lefts, seeming to gain strength with each blow. Musso fell back against the ropes. Bobby paused, as if to draw a bead on the target. He straightened Musso with a left, held him there. Then he threw the right. THERE’S NO BUSINESS




Manny Hyman had been in show business since he was sixteen. Four dec-ades of it and still he didn’t have a pot to puke in. He was working one of the lounges at the Sunset Hotel. The small lounge. He, Manny, was the “Comedy.” Vegas wasn’t what it used to be. The money had gone to At-lantic City where things were fresher, newer. Then, too, there was the god-damned recession.


“A recession,” he told them, “is when your wife runs off with somebody. A depression is when somebody brings her back. Somebody brought mine back. Now, there’s a laugh there somewhere and when I find it I’ll let you know….”


Manny sat in the dressing room nipping at a pint of vodka. He sat in front of the mirror…hair quite receded…shiny forehead, a nose that slid down and to the left…sad dark eyes…


Shit, he thought, I guess it’s tough for everybody. You keep slowing down but you have to keep going. Either that or it was your neck on the railroad track.
There was a rap on the door.
“Come in,” he said, “there’s nothing but quiet space here and some Jewish vegetation…”


It was Joe. Joe Silver. Joe booked the entertainment for the hotel, Joe pulled a chair over, sat down on it backwards, rested his arms and chin on the back of the chair and looked at Manny. Joe had been booking acts as long as Manny had been working them. They looked almost alike except that Joe didn’t look poor.
Joe sighed, stretched and rubbed the back of his neck.


“You go out there, Manny, and your stuff seems real bitter. Maybe you been in the game too long and it’s turning on you. You know, I can remem-ber when you were funny. You used to make me laugh. You even made the crowd laugh. It doesn’t seem so long ago…”


“Oh, yeah?” grinned Manny. “You mean last night, huh, Joe?” “I mean last year. I mean, I don’t remember when.”

MAD ENOUGH




It’s not exactly clear to me how it started. There had been a $5k advance and then a year or so had gone by and then I heard from Harry Flax that they were shooting the movie in Italy. Harry Flax was kind of a front runner, con man and pusher for the San Francisco publisher Waterbed Press. Harry Flax had his fingers in everything, including my bookcase from which some of my rare books had vanished, but that’s another story, and H.F. is not the only one who has dipped into my bookcase. Anyhow, I learned that they were shooting “Songs of the Suicide Man,” Luigi Bellini was directing, Ben Garabaldi was to play me and Eva Mutton was to be my woman. I seldom went to movies because I could murder my own hours, I didn’t need extra help. But the few people I knew told me that Bellini was a big number, had done a few odd and daring films. So they said. And over the years I had stumbled into some Garabaldi acting. He wasn’t bad. Not great but not bad. He had appealing eyes like a constipated man sitting on the pot straining to crap. I liked the eyes. But take that away and he was too comfortable. Nice macho guy, but self-pleased, not insane at all. Probably a great variety of ass had cooled him out. I didn’t know much about Eva Mutton but they told me she was a sleek sultry bit, that all the Italian men dreamed of cranking her.


O.K. I heard from Flax, heard from Hans Weiner my hit man and agent in Europe, so I knew the filming was under way. My short stories from Waterbed Press were being made into another form. So I forgot about it. I was working on my childhood novel The Band-Aid Dog and just thinking about my father and all those shits in grammar school who pissed on me, that was enough to think about.


So I typed and went to the track and when I wasn’t at the typewriter I was drinking with Sarah and although she only weighed a hundred and one pounds and I scaled at 225 she matched me drink for drink. Strange person to run a health food cafe. Anyhow, the horses ran lucky and the typing too and about three-quarters through the novel I got a phone call from Flax. He told me that the Italians were in town, they had to shoot some scenes at Venice Beach and they wanted to meet me. I told him O.K. and a time and place was set. Sarah liked the movie














362
scene. I was ready for the worst: stick your dick out the window and a blackbird will come along and light on it.


We parked outside the place in West Hollywood. Sarah and I got out of my car and Flax and his girl Sunday got out of theirs.


“Wait a minute,” I said, “we can’t go in there yet.” “Why not?” Flax asked.
“They might not have anything to drink.”
“Sure they will.”
“Can’t chance it.”


There was a liquor store across the street and I went in and purchased my own…


It was a large room with a row of tables covered with a long plank. The whole crew was there, Bellini, Garabaldi. Mutton was in Italy. She wasn’t in the Venice scene. Introductions were made. Lots and lots of Italians, most of them small and thin. Except for Bellini who was very short and very wide, and he had a nice human interesting face. Garabaldi was in bluejeans with a beard to look like me and I was clean-shaven with a Brooks Brothers coat, new slacks and shined shoes.


Somebody passed me a paper cup full of white wine. I drank it. It was warm.
“Is this all you’ve got?” I asked.
“Yes, yes, but there’s lots of it!”
“Christ, this stuff is WARM! You don’t drink white wine warm! What’s the matter with you people?”
“ICE!” somebody yelled. “GET SOME ICE! ICE!”
I unbagged my bottle of red wine, put the corkscrew to it, poured a drink for myself and Sarah, passed the bottle to the Italians.
“You ought to get some red,” I said.
“RED WINE!” somebody yelled. “GET SOME RED WINE!” Bellini looked at me. He was across the table. “Chinaski,” he said, “we’ll have a drinking contest.” I laughed.
He swung his right leg up and put it on the table.
“What the hell are you doing?” I asked.
“This is the way I drink.”
“O.K.” I swung my leg up on the table.
Bellini drained his drink. I drained mine. We got refills and drained them. It was going to be a great afternoon.

One of the Italians stuck a microphone in my face.


“Your mother sucks dog ears,” I told him.
He was a good boy. He laughed.


Ben Garabaldi was standing next to me. He just stood and held his wine cup in his hand. In an interview a year later he was to tell people that he had drunk me under the table. I suppose actors imagine whatever they want to.


“I saw you in that movie where you ran a nightclub,” I said to him. “Not bad.”
“I’m reading your books,” he smiled.


“Once I had a girlfriend who sculpted. She knew an actor who knew you and this actor had it set up so she was going to come see you and sculpt your head but I wouldn’t let her because I was afraid you were going to fuck her.”


He smiled again. He had a nice smile, full of knowledge. And those eyes.
But he wasn’t the right guy to play Chinaski. He was dozing inside.
Since the microphone was still there I answered some questions and told some stories and the drinking went on. The Italians laughed in all the right places while I talked. Sarah wandered off, she had heard all my shit. We drank on and on. I took off my coat and burned cigarette holes in my shirt. It wasn’t much of a drinking contest: the paper cups were too small and you had to keep pouring. At home I drank out of a silver chalice that held half a fifth. Soon there was nothing but white wine and warm beer left. I gathered Sarah, Flax and Sunday and we got out of there.


Months went by. Maybe a year. The novel got finished and I wondered if there’d ever be another one. Well, that didn’t matter. I still had the horses, the poem and the short story.


About this time I began to get letters from people saying that “Songs of the Suicide Man” was finished, that it was playing in Italy. Later I heard it was playing in Germany and then in France. I heard from a half dozen people who had seen it. The writer is damn near always last on the list. Who’s the writer anyhow? A writer is like a whore. You use a whore and then you are done with her.


They think that if writers suffer they will be all the better off for it. That’s crap. Suffering is just like anything else: get too much of it and you will eventually go under. Escape from suffering is what creates great writers: it feels so good that it makes the readers feel good.










364


Well, never mind. The film finally landed in Hollywood, it was to open in a theatre on Melrose. The phone began to ring. Too much. But it wasn’t Garabaldi or Bellini, it was the distributor and the friends of the distributor. This was a whole new gang. One guy, a publicist for the distributor, Benji, he wanted to interview me. He had a way of phoning at 8 a.m.
“No, Benji, no interview…”
“It will help the movie!”
“I’m not pushing the movie. I’ve heard it’s a piece of crap.”
“No, it’s great! It’s great! Let me just ask you a few questions about writing ‘The Suicide Man.’ It’ll help…”


“GOD DAMN YOU, BENJI, I’VE TOLD YOU TWICE THAT I WORK LATE AT NIGHT AND NEVER TO PHONE BEFORE NOON!”
“But by noon you’ve left for the racetrack!”
“You’ve got it.”
Click…


I found it works this way. At least in this case. The distributor buys the rights to show the movie, say English rights or American rights or European rights from the maker of the movie. Then the distributor tries to push it on the theatres to get back his investment. Anything he gets back over his in-vestment is his profit. And there are percentages in these deals. It seems to me to be a lot of pressure and one hell of a gamble.


One afternoon the distributor came by, with Benji, and three others. The distributor’s name was George Blackman and he was from New York and I like people from New York when I meet them in San Pedro, it’s when I meet them in New York that I get confounded. He was a big guy in a grey suit and necktie and Benji had on a grey suit and necktie, and I know these types, their shoes are always a little scuffed and the tips of their collars stick out. Blackman’s girlfriend was Angel and Angel’s hair was pure white and her face looked a thousand years old and yet the rest of her looked as if she were nineteen. That’s called coming through and they had all come through quite well, they had a rock-like charm but I didn’t quite know what to do with them. They brought bad wine in gallon jugs with handles and I put these in the broom closet and trotted out my good red. Sarah was curious and asked them a lot of questions which was good because it kept them away from me. Deals like this always involved drinking and eating and maybe drugs












365
and a certain relaxed atmosphere while overhead somewhere loomed the continual pressure and the desperation to stay alive throughout whatever the hell they thought they were doing.
“You get a percentage of this movie?” Blackman asked me.
“Supposed to, and don’t ask me if it’s a percentage of the net or the gross.
I don’t know.”
“All right,” said Blackman, “I won’t ask…”


Later they followed us in their car and we went down to the docks to the place where they have live crabs and the ovens. I liked it because the working men and women hung out there. Very few yuppies who can spoil any meal if you have to look at them. We walked along and looked in the crab tanks. “Now,” I told Blackman, “when you see the crab you like you just point it out to one of the boys and he’ll take it to the man at the oven for you.”


We selected our beauties and waited at a table with our beer. Somebody stuck a tape recorder in my face. It was Benji.


“Say something,” he commanded. “O.K.,” I said, “who’s paying for all this?” “Blackman.”


“Good, then try this: we are all trapped by circumstance and become crippled trying to escape.”
“Yeah?”


“Yeah. And there’s always some son of a bitch trying to give you the business on the freeway and he doesn’t know who you are and he doesn’t care. Worse than not caring: he’d just as soon kill you.”
“Yeah?”
“Yeah. Everything conspires and very little matters. And the big things seldom matter…”
“Yeah? What matters?”


“What matters are the small things like making sure you have enough water in your car radiator, or getting your toenails clipped, or having enough toilet paper, or an extra light bulb, things like that.”
“That doesn’t seem like much.”
“It’s plenty. Handle your trivial affairs well and the gigantic matters will fall into place.”
“Even death?”


“Even death will assume a perfect logic.” “I like that,” said Benji.
“I do too,” I said, “even if it might not be true.”










366


Then the crab was ready and we had more beer, then liked that so much we went for more crab from the ovens and more beer. It seemed lustful and lucky and then we packed back into the cars and back to my place.


We all met one more time after that. Blackman came over and baked some white fish heavy with onions and they brought some good red wine and we talked through the night and into the morning, it was something to do while waiting. Then the movie was ready. OPENING NIGHT….


Sarah and I had dinner across from the theatre. There it was, up on the marquee: “Songs of the Suicide Man.” We drank wine as we waited on the dinner. We had our own bottle of wine for the movie. I had an idea that we were going to need it. Many things filtered in between the book itself and the making of that book into a movie. Mostly big egos who wouldn’t let things alone, they had to interpret it according to their vision and their vision wasn’t very good or else they wouldn’t be dumb enough to waste themselves in the movie business.


We finished eating and trotted ourselves across to the theatre. There was a large crowd out front. We pushed into the lobby and then they surrounded me with their copies of Suicide Man. They each wanted an autograph. I had no idea so many copies were being sold. Where the hell were my royalties? It was hot in there and they jammed their copies at me. Sarah was pressed against me.


“This is worse than a poetry reading,” she said to me. “Nothing,” I told her, “is worse than a poetry reading.” Some guy handed me a pint of whiskey and I took a good hit. “Keep it,” the guy said, “your shit has given me a lot of laughs.”


So I hit at the pint again and kept signing books. Lots of young girls with my books. I figured they slipped them under their pillows at night. I went on and on signing and hitting the pint. Whiskey and wine are a good mix: utterly stupefying.


Now Benji was standing close by. He grabbed my arm. “They aren’t going to start the movie until you stop signing.” “THIS IS THE LAST BOOK!” I yelled.


We pushed in. Our seats were ready. We sat down and I reached into the bag and uncorked a bottle of red. Then it was dark and the movie began.














367


Ben Garabaldi was at a poetry reading. He was reading a poem. He had on dark shades. That was a bad beginning. As the movie rolled on it was much worse than I had expected. Garabaldi played his role as I dreaded he would: lax and terribly sane. As the scenes rolled on it got worse. Garabaldi kept sucking at his wine bottle but he didn’t drink like he needed it and he never got drunk. The purpose of wine is to get you drunk and make you forget. Well, Garabaldi forgot: he forgot to act. Then he meets Eva Mutton in a bar. I’ve been in hundreds of bars but I never saw a woman like that in a bar. She just wasn’t the bar type. She was more like a brooding fashion model who was unable to open her mouth.


The movie got so bad that I had to relieve myself. I started yelling things at the actors, giving them directions. But they wouldn’t obey me. I kept trying.


Finally some guy screamed at me, “WHY THE HELL DON’T YOU SHUT UP?” “I’M CHINASKI!” I screamed back, “IF ANYBODY’S GOT A RIGHT TO YELL
AT THIS MOVIE, IT’S ME!”
The movie rolled on and Garabaldi never did get drunk. At the end he’s down at the beach and he’s holding on to the legs of this teeny-bopper in a bathing suit. The waves break behind them and the wind blows through Garabaldi’s hair. He begins to recite a poem about the atom bomb which I wrote a couple of decades ago. He goes on about how pitiless and dumb we were to create the atom monster. He infers that we’ve done this to ourselves before in the long forgotten past, that more than once we’ve blown our chances to shit, and won’t we ever learn? Then he looks up the girl’s legs as the waves break and the gulls whirl.


“BLOW IT TO HELL!” I yelled and the movie ended to a tinkling of applause. We got out of there and I found myself in a bar with Blackman, Benji and crew. We were at a table and Sarah was suggesting that I shut up. Somehow, trying to be friendly, I had insulted the waiter. People wearied me, they were continually insulted; if you didn’t feed them what they
wanted to hear they took it as an affront.
We sat for some time and they just kept pumping up the movie and I started talking about other things, the horses and boxing matches but they just sat around pumping up the movie, not wanting it to fail because that meant they had failed. Tough.


















368


Next I knew, Sarah and I were out of there and driving along on the freeway but we were lost. I had no idea which freeway it was. But we still had wine and cigarettes. It started to rain. Vision was bad but not so bad that I couldn’t make out the sudden red lights in the rear view mirror. I pulled over.
I failed the breath test and next I knew my hands were behind me and the handcuffs were on. Then they had me stretch out in the road. The handcuffs bit into my wrists. I was in a river of water. It ran through my pants and into my shorts. There were 4, 5 or 6 cops in yellow raincoats. A couple of them were waving their flashlights about. They were talking to Sarah who wasn’t sober either.
“HEY!” I yelled up from the road, “I’M THE GREATEST WRITER OF THE 20TH
CENTURY! IS THIS HOW YOU TREAT YOUR IMMORTALS?” One of the cops walked over and put his flash on me. “You write, huh? What do you write?”
“Dirty stories. I’m up for the Nobel Prize.”


Then I was in the back seat of one of the cop cars with Sarah. One of the cops followed in my car.


“This is terrible,” said Sarah. “What’s going to happen to us?” She was not as used to the cops as I was.
“It’ll be all right,” I told her….


Next, it was very strange. I had blacked out. When I came to, the cuffs were off. Sarah and I were sitting in the front seat of my car. We were in a large parking area behind a police station somewhere.


“Sarah,” I asked, “where are the cops?” “I don’t know.”
I looked for my keys. My keys were gone. They’d taken my keys.
“Sarah, do you have my keys?”
“No. And they’ve taken my set too.”
I didn’t understand the whole procedure.


“Maybe we got a break,” I said. “Maybe they are going to let us sit here and sober up.”
“I think we should,” she said.
“Bullshit,” I said.
I was a key freak. I always carried an extra car key in one of my rear pockets. I only hoped it was still there. I reached back into that wet pocket and there it was!
“We’re saved,” I said. “We’re getting out of here!”








369


“No, no! I don’t want you driving like this! You’ll kill us!” She was freaked, the cops had her freaked.


I put the key into the ignition and turned the engine over. God, I felt great!
“No, no! Don’t do it!” said Sarah.
“Boy, are those guys going to be surprised!”
I pulled out of there and we were on the road and then I spotted a free-way entrance and we got onto the freeway.
“You’re going too fast!” Sarah screamed.
“Nonsense,” I said.
“TOO FAST! TOO FAST!”


I heard an awesome and terrible scream and then Sarah was upon me ripping my face with her fingernails and continuing to scream. I couldn’t ward her off. It was still raining and I had to hold tight to the wheel. She ripped at me furiously, finally relented, and we drove along. Then I began to see exit signs which I recognized. We were no longer lost. In fact, we were quite close to where we wanted to be. It wasn’t long before we were pulling into the driveway. Then, being a key freak I went to the glove compartment, found the house key and we were in. Sarah went straight up to bed. I sat downstairs dabbing at my face with a wet towel, still feeling high from the escape….


The next day I stuck my finger in the phone book and made an appoint-ment to get a tetanus shot. It was a long hall full of drunks and beat up guys. It was a merchant marine hangout. A girl handed me a long paper to fill out. I handed it right back.
“Aren’t you a seaman?” she asked.
“How do you spell that?” I asked.
There, she was affronted. I’d done it again.
I sat in a small room for 30 minutes. Then a nurse came in and gave me my shot.
“A woman did that, right?” she asked.
“Right.”
“You’ll go back to her, you’ll see.”
“I haven’t left.”


All that was over a year ago. I don’t hear any more about “Songs of the Suicide Man.” I suppose it’s shelved for good. But the scam is that it made a tremendous amount of money in Italy but I haven’t seen








370
a dime of my percentage. Meanwhile, another producer has come by. He’s from Spain. And he’s paid the advance. He wants to do five of my short stories and each one is going to be done by a different director, one from Spain, one from Germany, one from France, one from Japan and one from the U.S. Each in their own language. He came by one night and talked about it. I sat and drank through the night. He didn’t. It was very strange. I’ll let you know.
















































































371


tired in the afterdusk






smoking a cigarette and noting a mosquito who has flattened out against the wall and died


as organ music from centuries back plays through my black radio


as downstairs my wife watches a rented video on the VCR.


this is the space between spaces, this is when the ever-war relents for just a moment, this is when you consider the inconsiderate years:
the fight has been wearing…but, at times,
interesting, such as
resting quietly here in the
afterdusk as the sound of the centuries run
through my body…
this
old dog
resting in the shade
peaceful
but ready.






































372


swift and slow




sure enough, the ring


tightens.


I send out flares.


no
response.


this is no


surprise


only that I continue


is


especially


while knowing
that the end
is
there


and


here.

Luck




once


we were young
at this
machine…
drinking
smoking
typing


it was a most


splendid
miraculous
time


still


is


only now


instead of
moving toward
time
it
moves toward
us


makes each word


drill
into the
paper


clear


fast


hard
